        function gptClean(gptText) {

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="1eb6f2bef73b4050b4504c81d15b79a4">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '20b7264e-edd7-4b89-8381-6396bba407ea'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <h1 class="citation__title">Ultra-High-Energy Density in Layered Sodium-Ion Battery Cathodes through Balancing Lattice-Oxygen Activity and Reversibility</h1>

                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:shguo@nju.edu.cn"><span>shguo@nju.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Guo/Shaohua">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>
   </div>

and Reversibility link" href="https://doi.org/10.1002/adfm.202305470">https://doi.org/10.1002/adfm.202305470</a></div><div class="epub-section cited-by-count"><span>Citations: <a    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d129184002" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Lattice-oxygen redox in layered metal oxide cathodes offers a promising way to exploit high-energy density sodium-ion batteries. However, oxidation and reduction of lattice-oxygen are always asymmetric, showing poor reversibility upon charging and discharging due to the activated oxygen loss and subsequent structural rearrangement. Here, a layered Na<sub>0.7</sub>[Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.1</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (NLMCO) is developed by balancing lattice-oxygen activity and reversibility, which can deliver a record energy density of 729.7&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, further exceeding the state-of-the-art Na<sub>0.75</sub>[Li<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (NLMO, 638.4&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>). In light of electron paramagnetic resonance spectroscopy, in situ differential electrochemical mass spectroscopy, and electrochemical testing results, the highly activated lattice-oxygen is effectively stabilized in NLMCO without oxygen molecule release while obvious oxygen release is detected in the highly activated NLMO. Benefiting from the enhanced transition metal-oxygen covalency and reduced band energy gap, the NLMCO electrode demonstrates simultaneously high lattice-oxygen activity and reversibility, thus resulting in excellent rate and cycling performance, as well as ultra-high energy density. The findings highlight the critical association of energy density and lattice-oxygen redox reversibility, which will inspire more interest in anionic redox-based high-energy batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202305470-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202305470-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are a cost-effective option for grid-scale energy storage and low-speed vehicles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0001_R_d129183993e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0004_R_d129183993e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ. Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As one of the most critical components of SIBs, the cathode accounts for more than one-third cost of the whole battery and dominates the energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0005_R_d129183993e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Energy">5</a>, <a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0006_R_d129183993e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Storage Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Layered manganese-based oxide cathodes have been widely investigated due to their low cost and high theoretical capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0007_R_d129183993e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">7</a>-<a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0013_R_d129183993e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Inspired by lithium-rich layered oxides with two redox centers, namely cationic redox and anionic redox reactions, layered sodium manganese-based oxides are also explored by activating the anionic redox reaction to deliver the high reversible capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0014_R_d129183993e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>–</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0020_R_d129183993e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Energy">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The introduction of Li<sup>+</sup> into the transition metal layer accompanied by Na─O─Li configuration is considered to be a feasible way to trigger the redox activity of lattice-oxygen in layered SIB cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0021_R_d129183993e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Sci.">21</a>-<a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0023_R_d129183993e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Just like the Li─O─Li configuration in lithium-rich materials, the Na─O─Li configuration can also exhibit lattice-oxygen redox activity due to the non-bonding oxygen 2p orbitals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0024_R_d129183993e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Chem.">24</a>-<a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0027_R_d129183993e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nat. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, Na<sub>5/6</sub>[Li<sub>1/4</sub>Mn<sub>3/4</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> reported by Komaba et&nbsp;al. delivered a reversible specific capacity of 200 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> between 1.5 and 4.4&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0028_R_d129183993e602" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After that, other Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>[Li<i><sub>y</sub></i>Mn<sub>1−</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i>]O<sub>2</sub> (0 &lt; <i>x</i> ≤ 1, 0 &lt; <i>y</i> ≤ 1/3, e.g., Na<sub>0.6</sub>[Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.72</sub>[Li<sub>0.24</sub>Mn<sub>0.76</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, and Na[Li<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>) layered oxides with different crystal configurations were also explored to show a high capacity through lattice-oxygen redox.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0029_R_d129183993e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Environ. Sci.">29</a>-<a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0032_R_d129183993e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nat. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, it should be pointed out that the lattice-oxygen will also be oxidized to the undesired high valent state and react with electrolyte or release from the lattice, leading to subsequent structural rearrangement and even structural failure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0033_R_d129183993e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nat. Commun.">33</a>-<a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0037_R_d129183993e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nat. Energy">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Since the energy density is determined by the process of the reduction of oxidized lattice-oxygen during the discharge process rather than the process of lattice-oxygen oxidation during the charge process, high lattice-oxygen activity does not always mean high capacity or energy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0014_R_d129183993e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0038_R_d129183993e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the systematic comparison of Na<sub>0.6</sub>[Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> with ribbon superstructure and Na<sub>0.75</sub>[Li<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (NLMO) with honeycomb superstructure by Bruce et&nbsp;al. suggested that the optimization of crystal configuration and lattice-oxygen coordination environment could effectively regulate the energy efficiency of the cathode with lattice-oxygen redox activity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0039_R_d129183993e681" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nature">39</a>, <a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0040_R_d129183993e681" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nat. Energy">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally, although activating lattice-oxygen redox is crucial to enable layered cathodes to break the capacity limit, the irreversible redox, loss of lattice-oxygen, and structural rearrangement and failure are still the major obstacles that need to be overcome to realize high energy SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0041_R_d129183993e687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a>, <a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0042_R_d129183993e687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Herein, cobalt was successfully introduced into Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>[Li<i><sub>y</sub></i>Mn<sub>1−</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i>]O<sub>2</sub> system for the first time, and the developed Na<sub>0.7</sub>[Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.1</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (NLMCO) with the balance of lattice-oxygen activity and reversibility can reach an ultra-high energy density of 729.7&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, significantly surpassing the state-of-the-art NLMO (638.4&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>). Theoretical calculations reveal that the introduced cobalt can enhance TM─O covalency and reduce band energy gap, thus ensuring fast electron transport in TMO<sub>6</sub> slabs, and reversible generation and demise of the electron holes in lattice-oxygen during the charge/discharge process, respectively. Benefiting from the adjustment of electron structure, electron paramagnetic resonance spectroscopy (EPR), in situ differential electrochemical mass spectroscopy (DEMS), and galvanostatic charge–discharge results uncover that the oxidized lattice-oxygen with electron holes in NLMCO is effectively stabilized and no oxygen molecule release is detected, while oxygen molecule release appears in NLMO under high lattice-oxygen activation state, leading to higher lattice-oxygen reversibility of NLMCO than NLMO. Additionally, in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) shows that NLMCO demonstrates better structural stability, especially at the stage of lattice-oxygen oxidation than NLMO due to the robust TMO<sub>6</sub> framework of NLMCO. Finally, the electronic structure and crystal structure modulation of NLMCO ensures excellent redox reversibility under high lattice-oxygen activity, resulting in an ultra-high energy density.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202305470-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202305470-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>To successfully introduce cobalt into the Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>[Li<i><sub>y</sub></i>Mn<sub>1−</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i>]O<sub>2</sub> system, a solid-state phase reaction method with finely regulating the calcination and cooling processes was adopted to synthesize both NLMCO and NLMO. The actual composition was corroborated via inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES), and the detailed components (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) are consistent with the original design. By analyzing the Rietveld refinement results from synchrotron-based X-ray powder diffraction (sXRD) data shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0001">1a</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information, the structure of NLMCO can be well indexed to the hexagonal P2 structure with a space group of P6<sub>3</sub>/mmc.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0043_R_d129183993e762" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nano Energy">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, a superstructure diffraction peak at 11.2° can signify the honeycomb ordering of Li and TM. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information, although NLMCO, NLMO, and Na<sub>0.8</sub>[Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Co<sub>0.2</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> show the same phase, LiCoO<sub>2</sub> phase appears in Na<sub>0.8</sub>[Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Co<sub>0.2</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, suggesting more cobalt cannot be introduced into the structure. Neutron powder diffraction (NPD) was further conducted to confirm the structure of NLMCO, shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0001">1b</a>, and the Rietveld refinement results of NPD (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) are consistent with the sXRD results. The atomic-level structural characteristics of NLMCO were investigated via high-angle annular dark field (HAADF)-spherical aberration-corrected scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM), and TM arrangement is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0001">1c</a>. Energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) illustrates that the elements in NLMCO particles are well-distributed, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0001">1d</a>. The micromorphology of NLMCO is characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM), and the flake-like particles show a size of 1–3&nbsp;µm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202305470-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/48d531cb-e71e-4943-b8f4-1c1ee9ee6391/adfm202305470-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/48d531cb-e71e-4943-b8f4-1c1ee9ee6391/adfm202305470-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a85edc44-3c2c-42a5-901e-2c2895198383/adfm202305470-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/48d531cb-e71e-4943-b8f4-1c1ee9ee6391/adfm202305470-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202305470-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202305470" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) sXRD and b) NPD patterns of NLMCO. c) HAADF-STEM image projected along [100] direction and corresponding structure model (yellow: Na, blue: transition metals). d) EDS mapping of NLMCO.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The electrochemical performance of NLMCO and NLMO was investigated via galvanostatic charge/discharge in Na half cells. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0002">2a</a> intuitively compares the energy density of NLMCO and NLMO under the same testing condition of 0.1 C (1 C = 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>) in 1.5–4.5&nbsp;V, where NLMCO exhibits the record energy density of 729.7&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> in SIBs, better than that of NLMO (638.4&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>). The d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves of NLMCO and NLMO at 0.1 C are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information, from which it can be observed that the capacity during the charging process is predominantly supplied by the high-voltage stage, whereas during the discharging process, the capacity contribution is spread throughout the entire stage. The rate capabilities of NLMCO and NLMO were also evaluated, shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0002">2b</a>. Notably, the NLMCO electrode delivers higher capacities than the NLMO electrode at all the C-rates. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information, shows the charge–discharge curves of NLMCO at different rates. In detail, at 0.1 C, NLMCO and NLMO deliver a discharge capacity of 274.3 and 244.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Even at 10 C, NLMCO electrodes can still deliver a capacity of 164.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which is more than twice as much as NLMO (74.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). Generally, NLMCO also shows remarkably excellent rate capability compared with NLMO. This superior performance may be attributed to the increase in ionic and electronic conductivity, which facilitates timely Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction and electron transfer. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0002">2c</a> illustrates the cycling performance of NLMCO and NLMO electrodes at 5 C, where NLMCO and NLMO deliver discharge capacities of 173.1 and 147.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> during the first discharge. After 100 cycles, NLMCO demonstrates a capacity retention of 87.3%, while NLMO displays a capacity retention of 64.2%. The density of states (DOS) of NLMCO and NLMO are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0002">2d,e</a>. It can be found from the figure that the band gap of NLMCO is substantially lower than that of NLMO, suggesting a better electronic conductivity of NLMCO than NLMO. In addition, it can be found that NLMO exhibits a greater abundance of isolated O 2p states near the Fermi level in comparison to NLMCO, demonstrating that NLMO is equipped with high lattice-oxygen activity. Notably, NLMCO manifests an abundance of both O 2p and Co 3d states near the Fermi level, indicating the presence of strong Co─O covalency. The robust Co─O covalency generates a larger population of delocalized TM─O bonds, thereby enhancing lattice-oxygen stability and promoting the formation of stable oxidized lattice-oxygen intermediates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0044_R_d129183993e876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Energy Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, the reversibility of lattice-oxygen redox will be improved in NLMCO. The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was utilized to investigate the ionic conductivity of both electrodes. According to the sodium-ion diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) data evaluated by GITT in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0002">2f</a>,&nbsp;it&nbsp;is obvious that NLMCO exhibits superior <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> than NLMO during the initial charging stage accompanied by the oxidation process of lattice-oxygen. This can be taken as evidence that the kinetics of lattice-oxygen redox is strengthened. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0002">2g</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information, present an energy density summary of various representative SIBs cathode materials. Particularly, NLMCO exhibits the highest energy density among various SIB cathodes.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202305470-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/53ced229-7341-4c49-98a5-2806ba6f1572/adfm202305470-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/53ced229-7341-4c49-98a5-2806ba6f1572/adfm202305470-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9b8d1299-757d-48e0-88d6-4d8acdc6907d/adfm202305470-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/53ced229-7341-4c49-98a5-2806ba6f1572/adfm202305470-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202305470-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202305470" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Initial charge–discharge profiles and corresponding energy densities (shadow areas) of NLMCO and NLMO electrodes in 1.5–4.5&nbsp;V. b) Rate capability of NLMCO and NLMO electrodes. c) Cycling performance and Coulombic efficiency of NLMCO and NLMO at 5 C. DOS of d) NLMCO and e) NLMO. f) The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of NLMCO and NLMO for the initial cycle from GITT. g) Statistics of energy density for some representative SIB cathodes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The structural evolution of NLMCO and NLMO electrodes during charge and discharge in 1.5–4.5&nbsp;V was investigated by in situ XRD. The diffraction peaks of NLMCO (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0003">3a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) exhibit periodic changes without any new peaks upon charging and discharging, suggesting a reversible structural evolution. Additionally, even in full charge state, NLMCO still displays high peak intensity, indicative of a stable structure of NLMCO electrode in the wide voltage window. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0003">3b</a> displays the variation of lattice parameters of NLMCO from the refinement results of in situ XRD, where the (002) and (004) peaks are associated with the lattice parameter <i>c</i> and the lattice parameter <i>a</i>/<i>b</i> can be related with the (100) and (102) peaks. The ultra-low variation of lattice parameters <i>a</i>/<i>b</i> (1.284%), <i>c</i> (1.466%), and <i>V</i> (1.434%) further confirms superior structural stability and reversibility. As a comparison, the diffraction peaks of NLMO fade away in high voltage regions, shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information, which can be attributed to structural rearrangement derived from the irreversible lattice-oxygen redox (e.g., oxygen molecule release).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0045_R_d129183993e955" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Mater.">45</a>, <a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0046_R_d129183993e955" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Energy Environ. Sci.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally, cobalt doping can remarkably enhance the structural stability and reversibility to realize reversible lattice-oxygen redox. Additionally, regulating the lattice-oxygen redox chemistry can promote improvements in the crystal structure.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202305470-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b18be874-25d1-4b58-9132-4394ca822fbc/adfm202305470-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b18be874-25d1-4b58-9132-4394ca822fbc/adfm202305470-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d0d1ee73-851b-4b74-9f4b-f27dba3280fd/adfm202305470-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b18be874-25d1-4b58-9132-4394ca822fbc/adfm202305470-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202305470-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202305470" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) In situ XRD patterns of NLMCO. b) Variation of lattice parameters V (black points), c (blue points), and a/b (red points). c) Ex situ Mn K-edge and d) Co K-edge XANES spectra of NLMCO during the initial charge–discharge process.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Ex situ Mn and Co K-edge X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) measurements were also conducted to confirm the charge compensation mechanism, presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0003">3c,d</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0045_R_d129183993e986" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Mater.">45</a>, <a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0047_R_d129183993e986" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Sci.">47</a>, <a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0048_R_d129183993e986" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Chem. Rev. (Washington, DC, U. S.)">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is observed that the oxidation states of Mn and Co remain essentially unaltered during the initial charge process, implying that the specific capacity in this process is solely provided by lattice-oxygen oxidation, and original oxidation state of Mn is tetravalent and that of Co is beyond trivalence.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0049_R_d129183993e991" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Mater. Chem. A">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the subsequent discharge process, Na intercalation causes an ignorable energy shift of Mn/Co K-edge spectra around 3.5&nbsp;V, indicating no participation of Mn and Co. When the electrode was further discharged to 1.5&nbsp;V, Mn and Co K-edge spectra exhibited an obvious shift to low energy, suggesting the involvement of Mn and Co. Particularly, Mn<sup>4+</sup> is reduced to Mn<sup>3+</sup>, while Co<i><sup>n</sup></i><sup>+</sup> (<i>n</i> &gt; 3) is reduced to Co<sup>2+</sup>. The appearance of Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Co<sup>2+</sup> upon discharging confirms that a portion of the specific capacity is provided by Mn and Co in low-voltage regions. Ex situ O K-edge XAS test was also conducted to explore the lattice oxygen, and the results are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information.</p>
         
         <p>To further explore the underlying reason for the increased energy density of NLMCO, EPR measurement was employed to confirm the lattice-oxygen redox and the measurement was conducted at 110 K under vacuum. The EPR spectra of pristine NLMCO and NLMO electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information) are non-signaled. Additionally, ex situ Mn and Co <i>K</i>-edge XAS data (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0003">3c,d</a>) reveal that Mn and Co do not participate in the charge compensation during the initial charge process. Therefore, the peak intensity of EPR can be rationally attributed to the oxidation of lattice oxygen during the initial charge process. Shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0004">4a,b</a>, a salient signal (<i>g</i> = 2.004) is present in both fully charged NLMCO and NLMO electrodes but absent in the pristine electrodes, which can be attributed to the oxidized lattice-oxygen with a single unpaired electron such as (O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>3−</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0050_R_d129183993e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Nat. Commun.">50</a>, <a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0051_R_d129183993e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Particularly, NLMCO shows obviously stronger peak intensity than NLMO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0004">4a</a>) under the same condition, suggesting more oxidized lattice-oxygen with a single unpaired electron, not the oxygen molecule in fully charged NLMCO, which ensures that the oxidized lattice-oxygen can be reversibly reduced. In situ DEMS was employed to detect the formation of oxygen molecules in NLMCO and NLMO electrodes during the initial charge and discharge process. NLMCO electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0004">4c</a>) shows no oxygen release while obvious oxygen release appears in the NLMO electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0004">4d</a>) when the charge potential is beyond ≈4.2&nbsp;V, which is consistent with EPR results. Crystal orbital Hamilton population (COHP) was further conducted, shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0004">4e,f</a>, to analyze the TM─O covalency of NLMO and NLMCO.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0052_R_d129183993e1059" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Adv. Mater.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In COHP analysis, the positive value represents antibonding interaction, and the negative sign indicates bonding interaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0053_R_d129183993e1065" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Phys. Chem.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with NLMO, the bonding interaction of TM─O in NLMCO is enhanced below the Fermi level because of the introduction of cobalt. Additionally, it should be noted that the bonding interaction of TM─O in NLMO is weakened during the desodiation process while that in NLMCO is strengthened, suggesting an enhanced structure of NLMCO to stabilize lattice-oxygen oxidization during charging.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0053_R_d129183993e1070" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Phys. Chem.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The DOS results of NLMCO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0002">2d</a>)&nbsp;suggest that the bonding states should be connected with π-type interactions, leading to enhanced covalency, which is consistent with previous reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0054_R_d129183993e1078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Energy Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In NLMCO, the π-type interactions involve nonbonding O 2p and Co t2g states, which can effectively stabilize the nonbonding O 2p states and optimize lattice-oxygen redox. This analysis provides insight into the reason for introducing cobalt into the configuration, which can enhance the reversibility of lattice-oxygen redox.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202305470-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/66d09834-bb73-40fb-8c44-cd9deb670614/adfm202305470-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/66d09834-bb73-40fb-8c44-cd9deb670614/adfm202305470-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b2b49efa-9d5a-4bcd-972d-f33da4930abc/adfm202305470-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/66d09834-bb73-40fb-8c44-cd9deb670614/adfm202305470-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202305470-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202305470" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) EPR spectra of fully charged NLMCO and NLMO electrodes under the same measurement conditions. b) EPR spectra of pristine and fully charged NLMCO electrodes. In situ DEMS collected during the initial charge and discharge of c) NLMCO and d) NLMO electrodes. COHP profiles of e) Mn─O and Co─O bonds for NLMCO and f) Mn─O bonds for NLMO.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To comprehensively understand lattice-oxygen activation by Li and stabilization by Co in NLMO and NLMCO from microscopic atomic interactions to macroscopic electrochemical energy density, a schematic diagram with two models is presented in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0005">5</a>,&nbsp;in which the respective roles of Li and Co are elucidated and the intrinsic association of lattice-oxygen reversibility and energy density is also revealed. In detail, Na─O─Li configuration can be formed by introducing Li into TM layers, and the lattice-oxygen in the configuration exhibits redox activity because of the non-bonding oxygen 2p orbital. However, although the introduced Li in the TM layer can activate the lattice-oxygen, high lattice-oxygen activity does not always mean high energy density because lattice-oxygen loss (e.g., oxygen molecule release in NLMO) will occur during the initial charging process and energy density is dominated by the process of the reduction of oxidized lattice-oxygen (discharge capacity of oxidized lattice-oxygen O<sup>(2−</sup><i><sup>n</sup></i><sup>)−</sup>, 0 &lt; <i>n</i> &lt; 2) rather than the process of lattice-oxygen oxidation (charge capacity of pristine lattice-oxygen O<sup>2−</sup>). Fortunately, the introduced cobalt in NLMCO can enhance TM─O covalency (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0002">2d</a> and&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0004">4e</a>) to stabilize the oxidized lattice-oxygen (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0004">4a</a>) and suppress oxygen molecule release (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0004">4c</a>), leading to a high lattice-oxygen reversibility and discharge capacity. Particularly, although the discharge capacity of NLMO and NLMCO is contributed by lattice-oxygen and TM, the capacity offered by TM is similar and accounts for a small percentage (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202305470-fig-0002">2d</a>). Finally, a high energy density is obtained by balancing lattice-oxygen activity and reversibility in NLMCO.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202305470-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d5487ea6-6555-4628-b067-760462ac26a5/adfm202305470-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d5487ea6-6555-4628-b067-760462ac26a5/adfm202305470-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/41bb1b3f-a131-4549-a8b6-a071f0c1ca0b/adfm202305470-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d5487ea6-6555-4628-b067-760462ac26a5/adfm202305470-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202305470-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202305470" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagram of lattice-oxygen activation by Li and stabilization by Co in NLMO and NLMCO from microscopic atomic interactions to macroscopic electrochemical energy density.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202305470-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202305470-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, cobalt was successfully introduced into the classic Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>[Li<i><sub>y</sub></i>Mn<sub>1−</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i>]O<sub>2</sub> system for the first time. By balancing lattice-oxygen redox activity and reversibility, the as-prepared NLMCO can reach an ultra-high energy density of 729.7&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (the highest energy density among various SIB cathodes), higher than the state-of-the-art NLMO (638.4&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>). EPR, in situ DEMS, and electrochemical testing reveal that obvious oxygen release is detected in NLMO under a high lattice-oxygen activation state while the highly oxidized lattice-oxygen in NLMCO can be effectively stabilized without oxygen molecule release, leading to a higher lattice-oxygen reversibility of NLMCO than NLMO. Theoretical calculations suggest that the stabilization of highly oxidized lattice-oxygen can be attributed to the enhanced TM─O covalency in NLMCO. Additionally, the in situ XRD results show that NLMCO demonstrates better structural stability, especially at the stage of high lattice-oxygen oxidation than NLMO. Benefiting from electronic structure and crystal structure modulation, NLMCO shows simultaneously high lattice-oxygen activity and reversibility, resulting in ultra-high energy density, as well as excellent rate and cycling performance. Our findings highlight the critical role of lattice-oxygen redox reversibility in constructing high-energy batteries with anionic redox-based cathodes.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202305470-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202305470-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202305470-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202305470-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202305470-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202305470-sec-0070-title"> Materials Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>Both NLMCO and NLMO were synthesized by a traditional solid-state reaction method. The raw materials were Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, MnO<sub>2</sub>, and Co<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. The powder reagents Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and Co<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> were purchased from Macklin, and the powder reagents Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and MnO<sub>2</sub> were purchased from Aladdin. These raw materials were all weighed strictly according to the target stoichiometric ratio. To compensate for the volatilization of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> at high calcination temperature, an additional 5% was added. The weighed raw material powders were well mixed in a ball-milling pot for 5&nbsp;h under 300&nbsp;rpm. The well-mixed raw materials were calcined at 800&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h under oxygen flow, and both the heating and cooling rates were two degrees per minute.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202305470-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202305470-sec-0080-title"> Structure Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>XRD patterns and in situ XRD patterns were collected by a Bruker D8 Advance Diffractometer (Germany) with Cu Kα (<i>λ</i> = 1.5406 Å) radiation and GSAS2 was used to refine. A Hitachi SU8010 was used to investigate the particle size. In order to further characterize the material structure, the as-prepared samples were characterized by sXRD at the Powder Diffraction beamline and NPD using Echidna<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0055_R_d129183993e1244" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Phys. B">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> at the Open Pool Australian Light water (OPAL) research reactor at the Australian Nuclear Science and Technology Organization (ANSTO). The wavelength of the synchrotron X-ray and neutron beam were confirmed to be 0.77489(3) and 1.62193(1) Å by using the La11B6 National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) standard reference material 660b, and sXRD was recorded in the 2<i>θ</i> range of 3°–82.83° with a step size of 0.0039° using a MYTHEN microstrip detector.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202305470-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202305470-bib-0056_R_d129183993e1251" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - AIP Conf. Proc.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NPD data was recorded in the 2<i>θ</i> range of 4°–164° with a step size of 0.05°. In order to carry out an in situ XRD experiment in the process of the electrochemical cycle, a special cell with an aluminum film window was made for X-ray penetration. The in situ cell was cycled at a current of 0.2 C. The Mn and Co K-edge hard X-ray absorption spectroscopy (hXAS) spectra were performed at beamline 11B of the Shanghai Synchrotron Radiation Facility (SSRF, China). The O K-edge sXAS measurements were performed at beamline 11B of the SSRF (Shanghai). Electrodes with different states of charge were disassembled and thoroughly cleaned with dimethyl carbonate (DMC) to remove surface residues. For sXAS measurements, a homemade suitcase was used to transfer the sample directly from the glove box into the beamline without exposure to air, in order to avoid changes in the oxidation state of the cycling electrodes. The XAS spectra in TEY mode were first normalized by the incident beam intensity and then normalized to the front and rear edges of the absorption. ICP-OES (Avio500) was conducted to confirm the actual chemical compositions.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202305470-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202305470-sec-0090-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>The electrochemical tests were measured by the CR2032 coin-type cells, and sodium metal was used as anode. The NLMCO electrode with a mass ratio of active material, acetylene black, and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) of 70:20:10 was prepared. The positive electrode was dried under vacuum at 120 °C for 12&nbsp;h, and then the battery was assembled. The active material loading was 1.2–1.8&nbsp;mg, and the diameter of the electrode was 12&nbsp;mm. Cells were assembled in a glove box which was filled with high-purity argon (99.999%), and the electrolyte was 1&nbsp;mol L<sup>−1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in propylene carbonate, containing 5 vol% fluorinated ethylene carbonates as electrolyte additive. Glass fibers were used as separators for each sodium half-cell. The galvanostatic charge/discharge tests of half cells with different current densities were carried out at room temperature by using the Land BT2001A battery test system. 1 C of all electrochemical tests corresponded to 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202305470-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202305470-sec-0100-title"> Computational Methods</h4>
                  
                  <p>All DFT calculations were carried out using the Vienna Ab initio Simulation Package (VASP) program. The Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof exchange-correlational functional and the projector-augmented wave method at the generalized gradient approximation level were used. Ueff values were set to 4&nbsp;eV for Mn. The cutoff energy of the basis set was 400&nbsp;eV and the k-point mesh was 2 × 2 × 2. The residual Hellmann–Feynman force was 0.03&nbsp;eV Å<sup>−1</sup>. The COHP was calculated using the LOBSTER 4.1.0 code.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
